title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>)<br />see also: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Religion in the United Kingdom">Religion in the United Kingdom</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:left;">\n<hr /><div style="font-size:110%;">\n<p><span id="1">1</span>. People who identify of full or partial British ancestry born into that country. <br />\n<span id="2">2</span>. UK-born people who identify of British ancestry only. <br />\n<span id="3">3</span>. British citizens by way of residency in the <a href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="British overseas territories">British overseas territories</a>; however, not all have ancestry from the United Kingdom.<br />\n<span id="4">4</span>. British citizens or nationals.\n</p>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>British people</b>, or the <b>Britons</b>, are the citizens of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">British Overseas Territories</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dic62_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dic62-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a> governs modern British citizenship and nationality, which can be acquired, for instance, by descent from British nationals. When used in a historical context, "British" or "Britons" can refer to the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a>, the indigenous inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, whose surviving members are the modern <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dic62_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dic62-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> It may also refer to citizens of the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.\n</p><p>Although early assertions of being British date from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>, the creation of the united <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> in 1707 triggered a sense of British national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The notion of <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">Britishness</a> was forged during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> between Britain and the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a>, and developed further during the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Colley5_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley5-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The complex <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_formation_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the formation of the United Kingdom">history of the formation of the United Kingdom</a> created a "particular sense of <a href="/wiki/Nationhood" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationhood">nationhood</a> and belonging" in Great Britain and Ireland;<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Britishness became "superimposed on much older identities", of <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> cultures, whose distinctiveness still resists notions of a <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Homogeneity (ecology)">homogenised</a> British identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley6_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley6-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Because of longstanding ethno-sectarian divisions, British identity in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> is controversial, but it is held with strong conviction by <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Unionists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern Britons are descended mainly from the varied ethnic groups that settled in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> in and before the 11th century: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Prehistoric</a>, Brittonic, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The progressive political unification of the British Isles facilitated migration, cultural and linguistic exchange, and intermarriage between the peoples of England, Scotland and Wales during the late Middle Ages, <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Britain" title="Early modern Britain">early modern period</a> and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-Trudgill519_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trudgill519-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richardson531_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson531-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Since 1922 and earlier, there has been <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom_since_1922" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom since 1922">immigration to the United Kingdom</a> by people from what is now the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, mainland <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and elsewhere; they and their descendants are mostly British citizens, with some assuming a British, dual or <a href="/wiki/Hyphenated_identity" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyphenated identity">hyphenated identity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward113-115_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward113-115-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British are a diverse, <a href="/wiki/Multinational_state" title="Multinational state">multinational</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> multicultural and multilingual  society, with "strong regional accents, expressions and identities".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosen3_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen3-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Social_structure_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Social structure of the United Kingdom">social structure of the United Kingdom</a> has changed radically since the 19th century, with a decline in religious observance, enlargement of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a>, and particularly since the 1950s  <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Ethnic groups in the United Kingdom">increased ethnic diversity</a>. The population of the UK stands at around 62.5 million,<sup id="cite_ref-Eurostat_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eurostat-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/British_diaspora" title="British diaspora">British diaspora</a> of around 140 million concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BritDis47_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritDis47-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History_of_the_term"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Ancestral_roots"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestral roots</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a <span class="toctext">Geographic distribution</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Australia"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Australia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#British_overseas_territories"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">British overseas territories</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Canada"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Canada</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Chile"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">Chile</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#South_Africa"><span class="tocnumber">3.8</span> <span class="toctext">South Africa</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Ireland"><span class="tocnumber">3.9</span> <span class="toctext">Ireland</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Languages"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Languages</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Media_and_music"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Media and music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Visual_art_and_architecture"><span class="tocnumber">4.7</span> <span class="toctext">Visual art and architecture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Political_culture"><span class="tocnumber">4.8</span> <span class="toctext">Political culture</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_term">History of the term</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of the term">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Britain_(place_name)" title="Britain (place name)">Britain (place name)</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_names_for_the_British" title="Glossary of names for the British">Glossary of names for the British</a></div>\n<p>The earliest known reference to the inhabitants of Britain may have come from 4th century BC records of the voyage of <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> geographer who made a voyage of exploration around the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>. Although none of his own writings remain, writers during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> made much reference to them. Pytheas called the islands collectively <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xb1\xe1\xbc\xb1 \xce\x92\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xcf\x84\xcf\x84\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xce\xaf\xce\xb1\xce\xb9</span> (<i>hai Brettaniai</i>), which has been translated as the <i>Brittanic Isles</i>, and the peoples of what are today <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> of <i>Prettanike</i> were called the <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xce\xa0\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xcf\x84\xcf\x84\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xce\xbf\xce\xaf</span> (<i>Prettanoi</i>), <i>Priteni</i>, <i>Pritani</i> or <i>Pretani</i>.\n</p><p>The group included <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, which was referred to as <i>Ierne</i> (<i>Insula sacra</i> "sacred island" as the Greeks interpreted it) "inhabited by the different race of <i>Hiberni</i>" (<i>gens hibernorum</i>), and Britain as <i>insula Albionum</i>, "island of the Albions".<sup id="cite_ref-snyder_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snyder-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ohi_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohi-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Pritani</i> may have reached Pytheas from the <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gauls</a>, who possibly used it as their term for the inhabitants of the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-ohi_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohi-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> writers, in the 1st century BC and the 1st century AD, name the inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Cruthin" title="Cruthin">Priteni</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> the origin of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>Britanni</i>. It has been suggested that this name derives from a <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> description translated as "people of the forms", referring to the custom of tattooing or painting their bodies with blue woad made from <i><a href="/wiki/Isatis_tinctoria" title="Isatis tinctoria">Isatis tinctoria</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Parthenius_of_Nicaea" title="Parthenius of Nicaea">Parthenius</a>, a 1st-century<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Ancient Greek grammarian, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Etymologicum_Genuinum" title="Etymologicum Genuinum">Etymologicum Genuinum</a></i>, a 9th-century lexical encyclopaedia, mention a mythical character Bretannus (the Latinised form of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">\xce\x92\xcf\x81\xce\xb5\xcf\x84\xcf\x84\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xcf\x8c\xcf\x82</span>, <i>Brettan\xc3\xb3s</i>) as the father of <a href="/wiki/Celtine" title="Celtine">Celtine</a>, mother of Celtus, the eponymous ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Celts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 50 BC Greek geographers were using equivalents of <i>Prettanik\xc4\x93</i> as a collective name for the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> However, with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest of Britain</a> the Latin term <i><a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a></i> was used for the island of Great Britain, and later Roman-occupied Britain south of <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonia</a>, although the people of Caledonia and the north were also the self same Britons during the Roman period, the Gaels arriving four centuries later.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">end of Roman rule in Britain</a>, the island of Great Britain was left open to invasion by <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a>, seafaring warriors such as <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a>, who gained control in areas around the south east, and to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a>-speaking people migrating from what is today <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> to the north of Great Britain (modern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>), founding Gaelic kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alba" title="Alba">Alba</a>, which would eventually subsume the native Brittonic and Pictish kingdoms and become Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-sax1_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sax1-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">sub-Roman Britain</a>, as Anglo-Saxon culture spread across southern and eastern Britain and Gaelic through much of the north, the demonym "Briton" became restricted to the Brittonic-speaking inhabitants of what would later be called <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>), and parts of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Strathearn" title="Strathearn">Strathearn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morayshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Morayshire">Morayshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aberdeenshire" title="Aberdeenshire">Aberdeenshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde" title="Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SnowCelt_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SnowCelt-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In addition the term was also applied to <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> in what is today <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britonia" title="Britonia">Britonia</a> in north west <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, both regions having been colonised by Britons in the 5th century fleeing the Anglo-Saxon invasions.  However, the term Britannia persisted as the Latin name for the island. The <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">Historia Brittonum</a></i> claimed legendary origins as a prestigious <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> for <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Britons" title="King of the Britons">Brittonic kings</a>, followed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i> which popularised this pseudo-history to support the claims of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">Kings of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, and particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor period</a>, the term "British" was used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish people</a>. At that time, it was "the long held belief that these were the remaining descendants of the Britons and that they spoke \'<a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">the British tongue</a><span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>".<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> This notion was supported by texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Regum_Britanniae" title="Historia Regum Britanniae">Historia Regum Britanniae</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">pseudohistorical</a> account of ancient British history, written in the mid-12th century by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Historia Regum Britanniae</i> chronicled the lives of <a href="/wiki/List_of_legendary_kings_of_Britain" title="List of legendary kings of Britain">legendary kings of the Britons</a> in a narrative spanning 2000 years, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Trojans</a> founding the ancient British nation and continuing until the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain</a> in the 7th century forced the Britons to the west, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, and north, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde" title="Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a> and northern Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci1_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> This legendary Celtic history of Great Britain is known as the <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>. The Matter of Britain, a <a href="/wiki/National_myth" title="National myth">national myth</a>, was retold or reinterpreted in works by <a href="/wiki/Gerald_of_Wales" title="Gerald of Wales">Gerald of Wales</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cambro-Normans" title="Cambro-Normans">Cambro-Norman</a> chronicler who in the 12th and 13th centuries used the term British to refer to the people later known as the Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestral_roots">Ancestral roots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancestral roots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">Genetic history of the British Isles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historical_immigration_to_Great_Britain" title="Historical immigration to Great Britain">historical immigration to Great Britain</a></div>\n<p>The indigenous people of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> have a combination of <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Norse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-SnowCelt_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SnowCelt-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Parliament_1_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliament_1-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mebyon_Kernow_1_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebyon_Kernow_1-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RT\xc3\x89_1_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RT\xc3\x89_1-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_Government_1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_Government_1-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-United_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Between the 8th and 11th centuries, "three major cultural divisions" had emerged in Great Britain: the <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>, the earlier Brittonic Celtic polities in what are today England and Scotland having finally been absorbed into Anglo-Saxon England and Gaelic Scotland by the early 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth24-25_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth24-25-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The English had been unified under a single <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a> in 937 by King <a href="/wiki/Athelstan_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Athelstan of England">Athelstan of Wessex</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Before then, the English (known then in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> as the <i>Anglecynn</i>) were under the governance of independent Anglo-Saxon <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdoms</a> which gradually coalesced into a <a href="/wiki/Heptarchy" title="Heptarchy">Heptarchy</a> of seven powerful states, the most powerful of which were <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>. Scottish historian and archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Neil_Oliver" title="Neil Oliver">Neil Oliver</a> said that the Battle of Brunanburh would "define the shape of Britain into the modern era", it was a "showdown for two very different ethnic identities \xe2\x80\x93 a Norse Celtic alliance versus Anglo Saxon. It aimed to settle once and for all whether Britain would be controlled by a single imperial power or remain several separate independent kingdoms, a split in perceptions which is still very much with us today".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> However, historian <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama" title="Simon Schama">Simon Schama</a> suggested that it was <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a> who was solely "responsible for provoking the peoples of Britain into an awareness of their nationhood" in the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-SchamaNations_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SchamaNations-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scottish_national_identity" title="Scottish national identity">Scottish national identity</a>, "a complex amalgam" of <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Brittonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pictish" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish">Pictish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norsemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a> origins, was not finally forged until the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> in the late 13th and early 14th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arth_tapestry2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Arth_tapestry2.jpg/220px-Arth_tapestry2.jpg" width="220" height="386" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Arth_tapestry2.jpg/330px-Arth_tapestry2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Arth_tapestry2.jpg/440px-Arth_tapestry2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="1873" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arth_tapestry2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval tapestry showing <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, a legendary <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">ancient British</a> ruler who had a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Matter of Britain</a>, a <a href="/wiki/National_myth" title="National myth">national myth</a> used as propaganda for the ancestral origins of the <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a> and their <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British subjects</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Though <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> was conquered by England, and its legal system replaced by that of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535%E2%80%931542" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542">Laws in Wales Acts 1535\xe2\x80\x931542</a>, the Welsh endured as a <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> distinct from <a href="/wiki/The_English" class="mw-redirect" title="The English">the English</a>, and to some degree the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish people</a>, although conquered into England by the 11th century, also retained a distinct Brittonic identity and language.<sup id="cite_ref-Consci11_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consci11-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Later, with both an <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Scottish Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI of England</a>, under the counsel of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset</a>, advocated a union with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a>, joining England, Wales, and Scotland in a united Protestant Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ryrie82_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ryrie82-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Duke of Somerset supported the unification of the English, Welsh and Scots under the "indifferent old name of Britons" on the basis that their monarchies "both derived from a Pre-Roman British monarchy".<sup id="cite_ref-Ryrie82_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ryrie82-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the death of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a> in 1603, the throne of England was inherited by James VI, King of Scots, so that the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> were united in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> under <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James VI of Scotland and I of England</a>, an event referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> King James advocated full <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">political union</a> between England and Scotland,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and on 20 October 1604 proclaimed his assumption of the <a href="/wiki/Style_(manner_of_address)" title="Style (manner of address)">style</a> "King of Great Britain", though this title was rejected by both the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and so had no basis in either <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> or <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scots law</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_and_the_development_of_Britishness">Union and the development of Britishness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Union and the development of Britishness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britishness" title="Britishness">Britishness</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_formation_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the formation of the United Kingdom">History of the formation of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Great_Britain_(1707%E2%80%931800).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Great_Britain_(1707%E2%80%931800).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On 12 April 1606, the <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> representing the <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> between the Kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> was specified in a royal decree. The <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="St George&#39;s Cross">St George\'s Cross</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">St Andrew\'s saltire</a> were "joined together&#160;... to be published to our Subjects."<sup id="cite_ref-flaginst_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flaginst-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Despite centuries of military and religious conflict, the Kingdoms of England and Scotland had been "drawing increasingly together" since the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> of the 16th century and the Union of the Crowns in 1603.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley12_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley12-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A broadly shared language, island, monarch, religion and Bible (the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized King James Version</a>) further contributed to a growing cultural alliance between the two sovereign realms and their peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley12_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley12-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Parli_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parli-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688 resulted in a pair of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Acts_of_Parliamen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Acts of Parliamen (page does not exist)">Acts</a> of the English and Scottish legislatures\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689" title="Claim of Right Act 1689">Claim of Right Act 1689</a> respectively\xe2\x80\x94which ensured that the shared <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> of England and Scotland was held only by Protestants. Despite this, although popular with the monarchy and much of the aristocracy, attempts to unite the two states by Acts of Parliament in 1606, 1667, and 1689 were unsuccessful;<sup id="cite_ref-Parli_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parli-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> increased political management of Scottish affairs from England had led to "criticism", and strained Anglo-Scottish relations.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While English maritime explorations during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> gave new-found imperial power and wealth to the English and Welsh at the end of the 17th century, Scotland suffered from a long-standing weak economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In response, the Scottish kingdom, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William II of Scotland (III of England)</a>, commenced the <a href="/wiki/Darien_Scheme" class="mw-redirect" title="Darien Scheme">Darien Scheme</a>, an attempt to establish a Scottish imperial outlet\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colony</a> of New Caledonia\xe2\x80\x94on the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">isthmus of Panama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> However, through a combination of disease, Spanish hostility, Scottish mismanagement and opposition to the scheme by the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> and the English government (who did not want to provoke the Spanish into war)<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birnie_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birnie-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> this imperial venture ended in "catastrophic failure" with an estimated "25% of Scotland\'s total liquid capital" lost.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The events of the Darien Scheme, and the passing by the English Parliament of the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a> asserting the right to choose the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">order of succession</a> for English, Scottish and Irish thrones, escalated political hostilities between England and Scotland, and neutralised calls for a united British people. The Parliament of Scotland responded by passing the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Security_1704" title="Act of Security 1704">Act of Security 1704</a>, allowing it to appoint a different monarch to succeed to the Scottish crown from that of England, if it so wished.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The English political perspective was that the appointment of a <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobite</a> monarchy in Scotland opened up the possibility of a Franco-Scottish military conquest of England during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Second Hundred Years&#39; War">Second Hundred Years\' War</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The Parliament of England passed the <a href="/wiki/Alien_Act_1705" title="Alien Act 1705">Alien Act 1705</a>, which provided that Scottish nationals in England were to be treated as <a href="/wiki/Alien_(law)" title="Alien (law)">aliens</a> and estates held by Scots would be treated as alien property,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> whilst also restricting the <a href="/wiki/Import" title="Import">import</a> of Scottish products into England and its colonies (about half of Scotland\'s trade).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> However, the Act contained a provision that it would be suspended if the Parliament of Scotland entered into negotiations regarding the creation of a unified <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a>, which in turn would refund Scottish financial losses on the Darien Scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-Birnie_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birnie-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_of_Scotland_and_England">Union of Scotland and England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Union of Scotland and England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite opposition from within both Scotland<sup id="cite_ref-Scot18th_85-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scot18th-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and England,<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1213_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1213-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> was agreed in 1706 and was then ratified by the parliaments of both countries with the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a>. With effect from 1 May 1707, this created a new sovereign state called the "<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> This kingdom "began as a hostile merger", but led to a "full partnership in the most powerful <a href="/wiki/Going_concern" title="Going concern">going concern</a> in the world"; historian <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama" title="Simon Schama">Simon Schama</a> stated that "it was one of the most astonishing transformations in European history".<sup id="cite_ref-Schama_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After 1707, a British national identity began to develop, though it was initially resisted, particularly by the English.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1213_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1213-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The peoples of Great Britain had by the 1750s begun to assume a "layered identity": to think of themselves as simultaneously British and also Scottish, English, or Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley1213_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley1213-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Turner,_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_(1822).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg/220px-Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg/330px-Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg/440px-Turner%2C_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_%281822%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1269" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Turner,_The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_(1822).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Trafalgar_(painting)" title="The Battle of Trafalgar (painting)">The Battle of Trafalgar</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a> (oil on canvas, 1822\xe2\x80\x931824) combines events from several moments during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">Battle of Trafalgar</a>\xe2\x80\x94a major British naval victory upon which Britishness has drawn influence.</div></div></div>\n<p>The terms <a href="/wiki/North_Briton" title="North Briton">North Briton</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Briton" class="mw-redirect" title="South Briton">South Briton</a> were devised for the Scots and the English respectively, with the former gaining some preference in Scotland, particularly by the economists and philosophers of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, it was the "Scots [who] played key roles in shaping the contours of British identity";<sup id="cite_ref-Gottlieb15_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb15-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> "their scepticism about the Union allowed the Scots the space and time in which\nto dominate the construction of Britishness in its early crucial years",<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> drawing upon the notion of a shared "spirit of liberty common to both Saxon and Celt&#160;... against the usurpation of the Church of Rome".<sup id="cite_ref-Brad1_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad1-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Thomson_(poet)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Thomson (poet)">James Thomson</a> was a poet and playwright born to a <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> minister in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Scottish Lowlands</a> in 1700 who was interested in forging a common British culture and national identity in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-Brad1_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad1-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arne" title="Thomas Arne">Thomas Arne</a>, they wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Alfred_(opera)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred (opera)">Alfred</a></i>, an opera about <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>\'s victory against the <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Vikings</a> performed to <a href="/wiki/Frederick,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Frederick, Prince of Wales">Frederick, Prince of Wales</a> in 1740 to commemorate the accession of <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">George I</a> and the birthday of <a href="/wiki/Princess_Augusta_of_Great_Britain" title="Princess Augusta of Great Britain">Princess Augusta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Rule,_Britannia!" title="Rule, Britannia!">Rule, Britannia!</a>" was the climactic piece of the opera and quickly became a "<a href="/wiki/Jingoism" title="Jingoism">jingoistic</a>" British patriotic song celebrating "Britain\'s supremacy offshore".<sup id="cite_ref-Colley10_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley10-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island country</a> with a series of victories for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> associated empire and <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">naval warfare</a> "inextricably with ideals of Britishness and Britain\'s place in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColley1992132\xe2\x80\x93133_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColley1992132\xe2\x80\x93133-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a>, the new <a href="/wiki/National_personification" title="National personification">national personification</a> of Great Britain, was established in the 1750s as a representation of "nation and empire rather than any single national hero".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaleCopley199281_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaleCopley199281-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> On Britannia and British identity, historian Peter Borsay wrote: </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Up until 1797 Britannia was conventionally depicted holding a spear, but as a consequence of the increasingly prominent role of the Royal Navy in the war against the French, and of several spectacular victories, the spear was replaced by a trident... The navy had come to be seen...as the very bulwark of British liberty and the essence of what it was to be British.<sup id="cite_ref-borsay_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-borsay-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>From the Union of 1707 through to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a> in 1815, Great Britain was "involved in successive, very dangerous wars with Catholic France",<sup id="cite_ref-Colley18_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley18-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> but which "all brought enough military and naval victories&#160;... to flatter British pride".<sup id="cite_ref-Colley52_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley52-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> with the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a> advanced, "the English and Scottish learned to define themselves as similar primarily by virtue of not being French or Catholic".<sup id="cite_ref-Allan17_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allan17-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In combination with sea power and empire, the notion of Britishness became more "closely bound up with Protestantism",<sup id="cite_ref-Colley8_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley8-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> a cultural commonality through which the English, Scots and Welsh became "fused together, and remain[ed] so, despite their many cultural divergences".<sup id="cite_ref-Colley386_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley386-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The neo-classical monuments that proliferated at the end of the 18th century and the start of the 19th, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Kymin" title="The Kymin">The Kymin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Monmouth" title="Monmouth">Monmouth</a>, were attempts to meld the concepts of Britishness with the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman empires</a> of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. The new and expanding <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> provided "unprecedented opportunities for upward mobility and the accumulations of wealth", and so the "Scottish, Welsh and Irish populations were prepared to suppress nationalist issues on pragmatic grounds".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERojek20088_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERojek20088-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The British Empire was "crucial to the idea of a British identity and to the self-image of Britishness".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, the Scottish welcomed Britishness during the 19th century "for it offered a context within which they could hold on to their own identity whilst participating in, and benefiting from, the expansion of the [British] Empire".<sup id="cite_ref-Williams17_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the "new emphasis of Britishness was broadly welcomed by the Welsh who considered themselves to be the lineal descendants of the ancient Britons \xe2\x80\x93 a word that was still used to refer exclusively to the Welsh".<sup id="cite_ref-Williams17_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> For the English, however, by the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> their enthusiastic adoption of Britishness had meant that, for them, Britishness "meant the same as \'Englishness\'",<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaunce_et_al._200492_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaunce_et_al._200492-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> so much so that "Englishness and Britishness" and "\'England\' and \'Britain\' were used interchangeably in a variety of contexts".<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson34_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson34-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Britishness came to borrow<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> heavily from English political history because England had "always been the dominant component of the British Isles in terms of size, population and power"; <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> and hostility to <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a> were English factors that influenced British sensibilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Langlands_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langlands-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ichijo26_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ichijo26-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Ireland">Union with Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Union with Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">political union in 1800</a> of the predominantly Catholic <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> with Great Britain, coupled with the outbreak of peace with France in the early 19th century, challenged the previous century\'s concept of militant Protestant Britishness.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley322_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley322-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The new, expanded <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> meant that the state had to re-evaluate its position on the civil rights of Catholics, and extend its definition of Britishness to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howe40_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howe40-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Like the terms that had been invented at the time of the Acts of Union 1707, "<a href="/wiki/West_Brit" title="West Brit">West Briton</a>" was introduced for the Irish after 1800. In 1832 <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a>, an Irish politician who campaigned for <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a>, stated in Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">The people of Ireland</a> are ready to become a portion of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, provided they be made so in reality and not in name alone; they are ready to become a kind of West Briton if made so in benefits and justice; but if not, we are Irishmen again.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland_(1801%E2%80%931923)" title="History of Ireland (1801\xe2\x80\x931923)">Ireland, from 1801 to 1923</a>, was marked by a succession of economic and political mismanagement and neglect, which marginalised the Irish,<sup id="cite_ref-Howe40_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howe40-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> and advanced <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">Irish nationalism</a>. In the forty years that followed the Union, successive British governments grappled with the problems of governing a country which had as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, a staunch anti-Irish and anti-Catholic member of the Conservative party with a virulent racial and religious prejudice towards Ireland<sup id="cite_ref-Blake2012_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blake2012-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> put it in 1844, "a starving population, an absentee aristocracy, and an alien Church, and in addition the weakest executive in the world".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Although the vast majority of <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Unionists in Ireland</a> proclaimed themselves "simultaneously Irish and British", even for them there was a strain upon the adoption of Britishness after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nWar continued to be a unifying factor for the people of Great Britain: British jingoism re-emerged during the <a href="/wiki/Boer_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer Wars">Boer Wars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ward96_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward96-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward16_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward16-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The experience of military, political and economic power from the rise of the British Empire led to a very specific drive in artistic technique, taste and sensibility for Britishness.<sup id="cite_ref-taste_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taste-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In 1887, <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Harrison" title="Frederic Harrison">Frederic Harrison</a> wrote: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Morally, we Britons plant the British flag on every peak and pass; and wherever the Union Jack floats there we place the cardinal British institutions\xe2\x80\x94tea, tubs, sanitary appliances, lawn tennis, and churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson34_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson34-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Relief Act 1829">Catholic Relief Act 1829</a> reflected a "marked change in attitudes" in Great Britain towards Catholics and Catholicism.<sup id="cite_ref-Colley324_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley324-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> A "significant" example of this was the collaboration between <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Welby_Northmore_Pugin" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustus Welby Northmore Pugin">Augustus Welby Pugin</a>, an "ardent Roman Catholic" and son of a Frenchman, and Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a>, "a confirmed Protestant", in redesigning the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>\xe2\x80\x94"the building that most enshrines&#160;... Britain\'s national and imperial pre-tensions".<sup id="cite_ref-Colley324_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley324-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Protestantism gave way to <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> as the leading element of British national identity during the Victorian and <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian eras</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ward16_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward16-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and as such, a series of royal, imperial and national celebrations were introduced to the British people to assert imperial British culture and give themselves a sense of uniqueness, superiority and national consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward16_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward16-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Empire_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Empire Day">Empire Day</a> and jubilees of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> were introduced to the British <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ward16_127-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward16-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> but quickly "merged into a national \'tradition\'".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_period">Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom_since_1922" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom since 1922">Immigration to the United Kingdom since 1922</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:30a_Sammlung_Eybl_Gro%C3%9Fbritannien._Alfred_Leete_(1882%E2%80%931933)_Britons_(Kitchener)_wants_you_(Briten_Kitchener_braucht_Euch)._1914_(Nachdruck),_74_x_50_cm._(Slg.Nr._552).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/30a_Sammlung_Eybl_Gro%C3%9Fbritannien._Alfred_Leete_%281882%E2%80%931933%29_Britons_%28Kitchener%29_wants_you_%28Briten_Kitchener_braucht_Euch%29._1914_%28Nachdruck%29%2C_74_x_50_cm._%28Slg.Nr._552%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/30a_Sammlung_Eybl_Gro%C3%9Fbritannien._Alfred_Leete_%281882%E2%80%931933%29_Britons_%28Kitchener%29_wants_you_%28Briten_Kitchener_braucht_Euch%29._1914_%28Nachdruck%29%2C_74_x_50_cm._%28Slg.Nr._552%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/30a_Sammlung_Eybl_Gro%C3%9Fbritannien._Alfred_Leete_%281882%E2%80%931933%29_Britons_%28Kitchener%29_wants_you_%28Briten_Kitchener_braucht_Euch%29._1914_%28Nachdruck%29%2C_74_x_50_cm._%28Slg.Nr._552%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5981" data-file-height="8934" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:30a_Sammlung_Eybl_Gro%C3%9Fbritannien._Alfred_Leete_(1882%E2%80%931933)_Britons_(Kitchener)_wants_you_(Briten_Kitchener_braucht_Euch)._1914_(Nachdruck),_74_x_50_cm._(Slg.Nr._552).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A famous <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Lord_Kitchener_Wants_You" title="Lord Kitchener Wants You">recruitment poster</a>, stressing the concept of British national identity</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> "reinforced the sense of Britishness" and patriotism in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward96_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward96-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Through war service (including conscription in Great Britain), "the English, Welsh, Scots and Irish fought as British".<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The aftermath of the war institutionalised British national commemoration through <a href="/wiki/Remembrance_Sunday" title="Remembrance Sunday">Remembrance Sunday</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Poppy_Appeal" class="mw-redirect" title="Poppy Appeal">Poppy Appeal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn22_120-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn22-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> had a similar unifying effect upon the British people,<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn23_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn23-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> however, its outcome was to recondition Britishness on a basis of <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic values</a> and its marked contrast to <a href="/wiki/Europeanism" title="Europeanism">Europeanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn23_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn23-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Notions that the British "constituted an Island race, and that it stood for democracy were reinforced during the war and they were circulated in the country through <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>\'s speeches, history books and newspapers".<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn23_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn23-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At its international zenith, "Britishness joined peoples around the world in shared traditions and common loyalties that were strenuously maintained".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> But following the two world wars, the British Empire experienced rapid <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a>. The secession of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> from the United Kingdom meant that Britishness had lost "its Irish dimension" in 1922,<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn23_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn23-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and the shrinking empire supplanted by independence movements dwindled the appeal of British identity in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> during the mid-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/British_Nationality_Act_1948" title="British Nationality Act 1948">British Nationality Act 1948</a> and the subsequent mass <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom_since_1922" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom since 1922">immigration to the United Kingdom</a> from the Commonwealth and elsewhere in the world, "the expression and experience of cultural life in Britain has become fragmented and reshaped by the influences of gender, ethnicity, class and region".<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the United Kingdom\'s membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> in 1973 eroded the concept of Britishness as distinct from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> As such, since the 1970s "there has been a sense of crisis about what it has meant to be British",<sup id="cite_ref-Ward1_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward1-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> exacerbated by growing demands for greater political autonomy for <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn25_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn25-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The late 20th century saw major changes to the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Politics of the United Kingdom">politics of the United Kingdom</a> with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> national administrations for Northern Ireland, Scotland, and Wales following pre-legislative <a href="/wiki/Referendums_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Referendums in the United Kingdom">referendums</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Calls for greater autonomy for the four <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a> had existed since their original union with each other, but gathered pace in the 1960s and 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Spohn25_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn25-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Devolution has led to "increasingly assertive Scottish, Welsh and Irish national identities",<sup id="cite_ref-Ward180_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward180-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> resulting in more diverse cultural expressions of Britishness,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> or else its outright rejection: <a href="/wiki/Gwynfor_Evans" title="Gwynfor Evans">Gwynfor Evans</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh nationalist</a> politician active in the late 20th century, rebuffed Britishness as "a political synonym for Englishness which extends English culture over the Scots, Welsh and the Irish".<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London,_England,_UK,_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg/220px-Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg/330px-Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg/440px-Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London%2C_England%2C_UK%2C_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="958" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ve_Day_Celebrations_in_London,_England,_UK,_8_May_1945_D24587.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britons gathered in <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a> to hear <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>\'s victory speech on 8 May 1945</div></div></div><p>\nIn 2004 Sir <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Crick" title="Bernard Crick">Bernard Crick</a>, political theorist and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialist</a> tasked with developing the <a href="/wiki/Life_in_the_United_Kingdom_test" title="Life in the United Kingdom test">life in the United Kingdom test</a> said: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Britishness, to me, is an overarching political and legal concept: it signifies allegiance to the laws, government and broad moral and political concepts\xe2\x80\x94like tolerance and freedom of expression\xe2\x80\x94that hold the United Kingdom together.<sup id="cite_ref-Brad34_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad34-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> initiated a debate on British identity in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Brown\'s speech to the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a>\'s Britishness Conference proposed that British values demand a new constitutional settlement and symbols to represent a modern patriotism, including a new youth community service scheme and a <a href="/wiki/British_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="British Day">British Day</a> to celebrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> One of the central issues identified at the Fabian Society conference was how the English identity fits within the framework of a devolved United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> An expression of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">Her Majesty\'s Government</a>\'s initiative to promote Britishness was the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Veterans%27_Day_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Veterans&#39; Day (United Kingdom)">Veterans\' Day</a> which was first held on 27 June 2006.
