It boasts some of the oldest pieces of literature in the Western world, such as the epic poem <a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a>, one of the oldest surviving written work in the English language.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2008_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2008-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Famous authors include some of the world\'s most studied and praised writers. <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a> defined England\'s Elizabethan period.<sup id="cite_ref-Wagner2013_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wagner2013-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The British Romantic movement was one of the strongest and most recognisable in Europe. The poets <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a> were amongst the pioneers of Romanticism in literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray2013_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray2013-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Other Romantic writers that followed these figure further enhanced the profile of Romanticism in Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">John Keats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Snodgrass2009_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snodgrass2009-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Later periods like the Victorian Era saw a further flourishing of British writing, including <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Thackeray" class="mw-redirect" title="William Thackeray">William Thackeray</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dahiya1992_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dahiya1992-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Women\'s literature in Britain has had a long and often troubled history, with many female writers producing work under a pen name, such as <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Levine2001_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levine2001-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Other great female novelists that have contributed to world literature are <a href="/wiki/Frances_Burney" title="Frances Burney">Frances Burney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frances_Hodgson_Burnett" title="Frances Hodgson Burnett">Frances Hodgson Burnett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a> and the Bront\xc3\xab sisters, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Bront%C3%AB" title="Emily Bront\xc3\xab">Emily</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Bront%C3%AB" title="Charlotte Bront\xc3\xab">Charlotte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bront%C3%AB" title="Anne Bront\xc3\xab">Anne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SheenKozlowski2004_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SheenKozlowski2004-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Non-fiction has also played an important role in the history of British letters, with the first dictionary of the English language being produced and compiled by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, a graduate of Oxford University and a London resident.<sup id="cite_ref-Reddick1996_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reddick1996-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_music">Media and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Media and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Music of the United Kingdom">Music of the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Radio in the United Kingdom">Radio in the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Television_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Television in the United Kingdom">Television in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg/220px-Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg/330px-Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg/440px-Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Proms_in_the_Park_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Proms" title="The Proms">The Proms</a> is an eight-week summer season of daily orchestral classical music concerts held across the United Kingdom. The Last Night of the Proms celebrates British tradition with patriotic <a href="/wiki/Classical_music_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Classical music of the United Kingdom">classical music of the United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Although cinema, theatre, dance and live music are popular, the favourite pastime of the British is watching <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Public broadcast <a href="/wiki/Television_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Television in the United Kingdom">television in the United Kingdom</a> began in 1936, with the launch of the BBC Television Service (now <a href="/wiki/BBC_One" title="BBC One">BBC One</a>). In the United Kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a>, one must have a <a href="/wiki/Television_licensing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Television licensing in the United Kingdom">television licence</a> to legally receive any broadcast television service, from any source. This includes the commercial channels, cable and satellite transmissions, and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>. Revenue generated from the television licence is used to provide radio, television and Internet content for the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">British Broadcasting Corporation</a>, and Welsh language television programmes for <a href="/wiki/S4C" title="S4C">S4C</a>. The BBC, the common abbreviation of the British Broadcasting Corporation,<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> is the world\'s largest <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">broadcaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Unlike other broadcasters in the UK, it is a <a href="/wiki/Public_service_broadcasting_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Public service broadcasting in the United Kingdom">public service based</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">quasi-autonomous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statutory_corporation" title="Statutory corporation">statutory corporation</a> run by the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Trust" title="BBC Trust">BBC Trust</a>. <a href="/wiki/Free-to-air" title="Free-to-air">Free-to-air</a> terrestrial television channels available on a national basis are <a href="/wiki/BBC_One" title="BBC One">BBC One</a>, <a href="/wiki/BBC_Two" title="BBC Two">BBC Two</a>, <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_channel)" title="ITV (TV channel)">ITV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> (<a href="/wiki/S4C" title="S4C">S4C</a> in Wales), and <a href="/wiki/Five_(TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Five (TV channel)">Five</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_British_Television_Programmes" class="mw-redirect" title="100 Greatest British Television Programmes">100 Greatest British Television Programmes</a> was a list compiled by the <a href="/wiki/British_Film_Institute" title="British Film Institute">British Film Institute</a> in 2000, chosen by a poll of industry professionals, to determine what were the greatest British television programmes of any genre ever to have been screened.<sup id="cite_ref-greatestTV_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greatestTV-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Topping the list was <i><a href="/wiki/Fawlty_Towers" title="Fawlty Towers">Fawlty Towers</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/British_sitcom" title="British sitcom">British sitcom</a> set in a fictional <a href="/wiki/Torquay" title="Torquay">Torquay</a> hotel starring <a href="/wiki/John_Cleese" title="John Cleese">John Cleese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greatestTV_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greatestTV-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>"British musical tradition is essentially vocal",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECosman195722_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECosman195722-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> dominated by the <a href="/wiki/English_folk_music" title="English folk music">music of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic culture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> most greatly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hymns" class="mw-redirect" title="Hymns">hymns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglican_church_music" title="Anglican church music">Anglican church music</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> However, the specific, traditional <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Wales" title="Music of Wales">music of Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Scotland" title="Music of Scotland">music of Scotland</a> is distinct, and of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_music" title="Celtic music">Celtic musical tradition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, more people attend live music performances than football matches.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_rock" class="mw-redirect" title="British rock">British rock</a> was born in the mid-20th century out of the influence of <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a> from the United States. Major early exports were <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">The Kinks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Else74_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Else74-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Together with other bands from the United Kingdom, these constituted the <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a>, a popularisation of British pop and rock music in the United States. Into the 1970s <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2_tone_(type_of_music)" class="mw-redirect" title="2 tone (type of music)">2 tone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Else74_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Else74-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> is a subgenre of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> that emerged from the British <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">independent music</a> scene of the early 1990s and was characterised by bands reviving British guitar pop music of the 1960s and 1970s. Leading exponents of Britpop were <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(band)" title="Pulp (band)">Pulp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brit119_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit119-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Also popularised in the United Kingdom during the 1990s were several domestically produced varieties of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_dance_music" title="Electronic dance music">electronic dance music</a>; <a href="/wiki/Acid_house" title="Acid house">acid house</a>, <a href="/wiki/UK_hard_house" title="UK hard house">UK hard house</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jungle" title="Jungle">jungle</a>, <a href="/wiki/UK_garage" title="UK garage">UK garage</a> which in turn have influenced <a href="/wiki/Grime_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grime (music)">grime</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_hip_hop" title="British hip hop">British hip hop</a> in the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-Brit119_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit119-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_BRIT_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="The BRIT Awards">The BRIT Awards</a> are the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a>\'s annual awards for both international and <a href="/wiki/British_popular_music" title="British popular music">British popular music</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Religion in the United Kingdom">Religion in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/170px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/255px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/340px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> is used for the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">coronation of the British monarchs</a>, who are also made the head of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> has been the most influential and important religion in Britain, and it remains the declared faith of the majority of the British people.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001234_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001234-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> The influence of Christianity on British culture has been "widespread, extending beyond the spheres of prayer and worship. Churches and cathedrals make a significant contribution to the architectural landscape of the nation\'s cities and towns" whilst "many schools and hospitals were founded by men and women who were strongly influenced by Christian motives".<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001234_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001234-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, the "two most important events in the Christian calendar", are recognised as <a href="/wiki/Holiday" title="Holiday">public holidays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001234_229-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001234-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Christianity remains the major religion of the population of the United Kingdom in the 21st century, followed by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> in terms of numbers of adherents. The 2007 <a href="/wiki/Tearfund" title="Tearfund">Tearfund</a> Survey revealed 53% identified themselves as Christian, which was similar to the 2004 <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes_Survey" title="British Social Attitudes Survey">British Social Attitudes Survey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Tearfund_Survey_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tearfund_Survey-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BSAS2004_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BSAS2004-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> and to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">United Kingdom Census 2001</a> in which 71.6% said that Christianity was their religion,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> However, the Tearfund Survey showed only one in ten Britons attend church weekly.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">Secularism</a> was advanced in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, and modern British organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/British_Humanist_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="British Humanist Association">British Humanist Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Secular_Society" title="National Secular Society">National Secular Society</a> offer the opportunity for their members to "debate and explore the moral and philosophical issues in a non-religious setting".<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001234_229-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001234-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> that led to the formation of the Kingdom of Great Britain ensured that there would be a <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> succession as well as a link between <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">church and state</a> that still remains. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (<a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>) is legally recognised as the <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a>, and so retains representation in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</a>, whilst the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">British monarch</a> is a member of the church as well as its <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001235_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001235-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The Church of England also retains the right to draft legislative measures (related to religious administration) through the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod</a> that can then be passed into law by Parliament. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church_in_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church in England and Wales">Roman Catholic Church in England and Wales</a> is the second largest Christian church with around five million members, mainly in England.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> There are also growing <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostal</a> churches, with Pentecostal churches in England now third after the Church of England and the Roman Catholic Church in terms of church attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Other large Christian groups include <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> (known informally as <a href="/wiki/The_Kirk" class="mw-redirect" title="The Kirk">The Kirk</a>), is recognised as the <a href="/wiki/National_church" title="National church">national church</a> of Scotland and not subject to state control. The British monarch is an ordinary member and is required to swear an oath to "defend the security" of the church upon his or her accession. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Scotland">Roman Catholic Church in Scotland</a> is Scotland\'s second largest Christian church, with followers representing a sixth of the population of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Scottish Episcopal Church</a>, which is part of the Anglican Communion, dates from the final establishment of Presbyterianism in Scotland in 1690, when it split from the Church of Scotland over matters of theology and ritual. Further splits in the Church of Scotland, especially in the 19th century, led to the creation of other Presbyterian churches in Scotland, including the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(post-1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (post-1900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>. In the 1920s, the <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> became independent from the Church of England and became \'<a href="/wiki/Disestablishmentarianism" title="Disestablishmentarianism">disestablished\'</a> but remains in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001235_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001235-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Methodism and other Protestant churches have had a major presence in Wales. The main <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Religion in Northern Ireland">religious groups in Northern Ireland</a> are organised on an <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland" title="All-Ireland">all-Ireland</a> basis. Though collectively Protestants constitute the overall majority,<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Ireland">Roman Catholic Church of Ireland</a> is the largest single church. The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church in Ireland</a>, closely linked to the Church of Scotland in terms of theology and history, is the second largest church followed by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> (Anglican) which was disestablished in the 19th century.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Sport in the United Kingdom">Sport in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Home_Nations" title="Home Nations">Home Nations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg/220px-GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg/330px-GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg/440px-GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GB_Gold_WOC2008_Relay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The British gold medalist relay team of the 2008 <a href="/wiki/World_Orienteering_Championships" title="World Orienteering Championships">World Orienteering Championships</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sport" title="Sport">Sport</a> is an important element of British culture, and is one of the most popular leisure activities of Britons. Within the United Kingdom, nearly half of all adults partake in one or more sporting activity each week.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001282_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001282-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Some of the major sports in the United Kingdom "were invented by the British",<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001293_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001293-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricket</a>, and "exported various other games" including <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badminton" title="Badminton">badminton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snooker" title="Snooker">snooker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Misc164_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Misc164-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In most sports, separate organisations, teams and clubs represent the individual <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a> at international level, though in some sports, like rugby union, an all-Ireland team represents both Northern Ireland and the Republic, and the <a href="/wiki/British_and_Irish_Lions" title="British and Irish Lions">British and Irish Lions</a> represent the isles as a whole. The UK is represented by a single team at the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> and at the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_at_the_Olympics" title="Great Britain at the Olympics">Great Britain team</a> won 65 medals: 29 gold (the most since the <a href="/wiki/1908_Summer_Olympics" title="1908 Summer Olympics">1908 Summer Olympics</a>), 17 silver and 19 bronze, ranking them 3rd.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> In total, sportsmen and women from the UK "hold over 50 world titles in a variety of sports, such as professional boxing, rowing, snooker, squash and motorcycle sports".<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001282_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001282-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2006 poll found that association football was the most popular sport in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> In England 320&#160;football clubs are affiliated to <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">The Football Association</a> (FA) and more than 42,000 clubs to regional or district associations. The FA, founded in 1863, and the Football League, founded in 1888, were both the first of their kind in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001297_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001297-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In Scotland there are 78 full and associate clubs and nearly 6,000 registered clubs under the jurisdiction of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Football_Association" title="Scottish Football Association">Scottish Football Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001297_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001297-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Two Welsh clubs play in England\'s Football League, one in the Premier league, and others at non-league level, whilst the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Football_League" title="Welsh Football League">Welsh Football League</a> contains 20 semi-professional clubs. In Northern Ireland, 12 semi-professional clubs play in the <a href="/wiki/IFA_Premiership" class="mw-redirect" title="IFA Premiership">IFA Premiership</a>, the second oldest league in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001297_245-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001297-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Recreational_fishing" title="Recreational fishing">Recreational fishing</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Angling" title="Angling">angling</a>, is one of the most popular participation activities in the United Kingdom, with an estimated 3\xe2\x80\x944 million anglers in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001293_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001293-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The most widely practised form of angling in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> is for <a href="/wiki/Coarse_fishing" title="Coarse fishing">coarse fish</a> while in Scotland angling is usually for <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Briton2001293_241-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briton2001293-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_art_and_architecture">Visual art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Visual art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Architecture of the United Kingdom">Architecture of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Art of the United Kingdom">Art of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>For centuries, artists and architects in Britain were overwhelmingly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Western_art_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Western art history">Western art history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Else78_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Else78-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Amongst the first visual artists credited for developing a distinctly British aesthetic and artistic style is <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Else78_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Else78-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The experience of military, political and economic power from the rise of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, led to a very specific drive in artistic technique, taste and sensibility in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-taste_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taste-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Britons used their art "to illustrate their knowledge and command of the natural world", whilst the permanent settlers in British North America, Australasia, and South Africa "embarked upon a search for distinctive artistic expression appropriate to their sense of national identity".<sup id="cite_ref-taste_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taste-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The empire has been "at the centre, rather than in the margins, of the history of British art", and imperial British visual arts have been fundamental to the construction, celebration and expression of Britishness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarringer_et_al._20073_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarringer_et_al._20073-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British attitudes to <a href="/wiki/Modern_art" title="Modern art">modern art</a> were "polarised" at the end of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Whittle5_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whittle5-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Modernist movements were both cherished and vilified by artists and critics; <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">Impressionism</a> was initially regarded by "many conservative critics" as a "subversive foreign influence", but became "fully assimilated" into British art during the early-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Whittle5_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whittle5-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Representation_(arts)" title="Representation (arts)">Representational art</a> was described by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Read" title="Herbert Read">Herbert Read</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar period</a> as "necessarily... revolutionary", and was studied and produced to such an extent that by the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Classicism" title="Classicism">Classicism</a> was effectively void in British visual art.<sup id="cite_ref-Whittle5_249-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whittle5-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_art" title="Postmodern art">Post-modern</a>, contemporary British art, particularly that of the <a href="/wiki/Young_British_Artists" title="Young British Artists">Young British Artists</a>, has been pre-occupied with <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">postcolonialism</a>, and "characterised by a fundamental concern with material culture&#160;... perceived as a post-imperial cultural anxiety".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarringer_et_al._200717_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarringer_et_al._200717-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Architecture of the United Kingdom">Architecture of the United Kingdom</a> is diverse; most influential developments have usually taken place in England, but Ireland, Scotland, and Wales have at various times played leading roles in architectural history.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Although there are prehistoric and classical structures in the British Isles, British architecture effectively begins with the first Anglo-Saxon Christian churches, built soon after <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a> arrived in Great Britain in 597.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman architecture</a> was built on a vast scale from the 11th century onwards in the form of castles and churches to help impose Norman authority upon their dominion.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart_251-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a>, which flourished between 1180 until around 1520, was initially imported from France, but quickly developed its own unique qualities.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart_251-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Secular <a href="/wiki/Medieval_architecture" title="Medieval architecture">medieval architecture</a> throughout Britain has left a legacy of large stone <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a>, with the "finest examples" being found lining both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Scottish_border" title="Anglo-Scottish border">Anglo-Scottish border</a>, dating from the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a> of the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart2_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart2-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> The invention of gunpowder and canons made castles redundant, and the <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance" title="English Renaissance">English Renaissance</a> which followed facilitiated the development of new artistic styles for domestic architecture: <a href="/wiki/Tudor_style_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor style architecture">Tudor style</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Baroque" title="English Baroque">English Baroque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_Style_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Anne Style architecture">The Queen Anne Style</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palladian_architecture" title="Palladian architecture">Palladian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BritEncart2_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritEncart2-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical architecture</a> advanced after the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a>. Outwith the United Kingdom, the influence of British architecture is particularly strong in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sing69_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sing69-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> the result of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British rule in India</a> in the 19th century. The Indian cities of <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> each have courts, hotels and <a href="/wiki/Train_station" title="Train station">train stations</a> designed in British architectural styles of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic Revivalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">neoclassicism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sing69_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sing69-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">Political culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Political culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Politics of the United Kingdom">Politics of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Westminster,_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/220px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" width="220" height="87" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/330px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg/440px-Palace_of_Westminster%2C_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="1261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palace_of_Westminster,_London_-_Feb_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a> is a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> which houses the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a>.</div></div></div><p>\nBritish political culture is tied closely with its institutions and <a href="/wiki/Civics" title="Civics">civics</a>, and a "subtle fusion of new and old values".<sup id="cite_ref-Craith169_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craith169-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> The principle of <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, with its notions of stable <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary government">parliamentary government</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_liberalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Political liberalism">political liberalism</a>, "have come to dominate British culture".<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> These views have been reinforced by Sir <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Crick" title="Bernard Crick">Bernard Crick</a> who said:<sup id="cite_ref-Brad34_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad34-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To be British seems to us to mean that we respect the laws, the elected parliamentary and democratic political structures, traditional values of mutual tolerance, respect for equal rights and mutual concern; that we give our allegiance to the state (as commonly symbolised by <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a>) in return for its protection.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>British political institutions include the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Council of the United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Although the Privy Council is primarily a British institution, officials from other Commonwealth realms are also appointed to the body.<sup id="cite_ref-gay-p3_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gay-p3-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> The most notable continuing instance is the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_New_Zealand" title="Prime Minister of New Zealand">Prime Minister of New Zealand</a>, its senior politicians, Chief Justice and Court of Appeal judges are conventionally made Privy Counsellors,<sup id="cite_ref-nz-pc_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nz-pc-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> as the prime ministers and chief justices of Canada and Australia used to be.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Prime Ministers of Commonwealth countries which retain the <a href="/wiki/British_monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarch">British monarch</a> as their sovereign continue to be sworn as Privy Counsellors.<sup id="cite_ref-gay-p3_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gay-p3-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">Universal suffrage</a> for all males over 21 was granted in 1918 and for adult women in 1928 after the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Women&#39;s suffrage in the United Kingdom">Suffragette movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Politics in the United Kingdom is <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party</a>, with three dominant political parties: the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Social_structure_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Social structure of Britain">social structure of Britain</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>, has "long been pre-eminent among the factors used to explain party allegiance", and still persists as "the dominant basis" of party political allegiance for Britons.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The Conservative Party is descended from the historic <a href="/wiki/Tory_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Tory Party">Tory Party</a> (founded in England in 1678), and is a <a href="/wiki/Centre-right" class="mw-redirect" title="Centre-right">centre-right</a> <a href="/wiki/Conservative" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative">conservative</a> political party,<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> which traditionally draws support from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle classes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> The Labour Party (founded by Scotsman <a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a>) grew out of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> movement and <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> political parties of the 19th century, and continues to describe itself as a "democratic socialist party".<sup id="cite_ref-iqgxtf_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iqgxtf-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Labour states that it stands for the representation of the low-paid <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a>, who have traditionally been its members and voters.<sup id="cite_ref-iqgxtf_265-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iqgxtf-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> is the third largest political party in the UK in terms of both party membership and representation in parliament, having won 56 out of 59 Scottish seats at the 2015 General Election. The <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> are a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> political party, and third largest in England in terms of membership and MPs elected. It is descended from the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, a major <a href="/wiki/Ruling_party" title="Ruling party">ruling party</a> of 19th-century Britain through to the First World War, when it was supplanted by the Labour Party.<sup id="cite_ref-Dear102_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dear102-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> The Liberal Democrats have historically drawn support from wide and "differing social backgrounds".<sup id="cite_ref-Dear102_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dear102-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> There are over 300 other, smaller <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">political parties in the United Kingdom</a> registered to the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_Kingdom)" title="Electoral Commission (United Kingdom)">Electoral Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Ethnic groups in the United Kingdom">Ethnic groups in the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001_Ethnic_Codes" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001 Ethnic Codes">United Kingdom Census 2001 Ethnic Codes</a></div>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes_Survey" title="British Social Attitudes Survey">British Social Attitudes Survey</a>, there are broadly two interpretations of British identity, with ethnic and civic dimensions:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The first group, which we term the ethnic dimension, contained the items about birthplace, ancestry, living in Britain, and sharing British customs and traditions. The second, or civic group, contained the items about feeling British, respecting laws and institutions, speaking English, and having British citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Of the two perspectives of British identity, the civic definition has become "the dominant idea&#160;... by far",<sup id="cite_ref-Ichijo26_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ichijo26-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and in this capacity, Britishness is sometimes considered an institutional or overarching <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a> identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Langlands_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langlands-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ichijo26_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ichijo26-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brad34_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad34-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> This has been used to explain why first-, second- and third-generation immigrants are more likely to describe themselves as British, rather than English, because it is an "institutional, inclusive" identity, that can be acquired through <a href="/wiki/Naturalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalisation">naturalisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Ichijo27_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ichijo27-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> the vast majority of people in the United Kingdom who are from an ethnic minority feel British.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, this attitude is more common in England than in Scotland or Wales; "white English people perceived themselves as English first and as British second, and most people from ethnic minority backgrounds perceived themselves as British, but none identified as English, a label they associated exclusively with white people". Contrawise, in Scotland and Wales, <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">White British</a> and ethnic minority people both identified more strongly with Scotland and Wales than with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies and surveys have "reported that the majority of the Scots and Welsh see themselves as both Scottish/Welsh and British though with some differences in emphasis".<sup id="cite_ref-Ichijo27_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ichijo27-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Racial_Equality" title="Commission for Racial Equality">Commission for Racial Equality</a> found that with respect to notions of <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">nationality</a> in Britain, "the most basic, objective and uncontroversial conception of the British people is one that includes the English, the Scots and the Welsh".<sup id="cite_ref-CRE22_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRE22-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> However, "English participants tended to think of themselves as indistinguishably English or British, while both Scottish and Welsh participants identified themselves much more readily as Scottish or Welsh than as British".<sup id="cite_ref-CRE22_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRE22-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some persons opted "to combine both identities" as "they felt Scottish or Welsh, but held a <a href="/wiki/British_passport" title="British passport">British passport</a> and were therefore British", whereas others saw themselves as exclusively Scottish or exclusively Welsh and "felt quite divorced from the British, whom they saw as the English".<sup id="cite_ref-CRE22_273-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRE22-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Commentators have described this latter phenomenon as "<a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>", a rejection of British identity because some Scots and Welsh interpret it as "cultural imperialism imposed" upon the United Kingdom by "English ruling elites",<sup id="cite_ref-Ward2-3_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward2-3-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> or else a response to a historical misappropriation of equating the word "English" with "British",<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> which has "brought about a desire among Scots, Welsh and Irish to learn more about their heritage and distinguish themselves from the broader British identity".<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anti-British_sentiment" title="Anti-British sentiment">Anti-British sentiment</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Demographics of the United Kingdom">Demographics of the United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_British_people" title="Lists of British people">Lists of British people</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=British_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-citation_style" class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a> reported in 2006 that 6,570,015 Canadians identified themselves as having English ancestry, 4,719,850 Scottish, 440,965 Welsh and 403,915 from the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> (other than English, Scottish, Welsh or Irish). See: <br /> <cite id="CITEREFStatistics_Canada2006" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a> (2006), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www12.statcan.ca/english/census06/data/highlights/ethnic/pages/Page.cfm?Lang=E&amp;Geo=PR&amp;Code=01&amp;Data=Count&amp;Table=2&amp;StartRec=1&amp;Sort=3&amp;Display=All&amp;CSDFilter=5000">"Ethnic origins, 2006 counts, for Canada, provinces and territories"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Canada_2006_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2006 Census">Canada 2006 Census</a></i>, statcan.ca<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 May</span> 2009</span></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Canada+2006+Census&amp;rft.atitle=Ethnic+origins%2C+2006+counts%2C+for+Canada%2C+provinces+and+territories&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.au=Statistics+Canada&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww12.statcan.ca%2Fenglish%2Fcensus06%2Fdata%2Fhighlights%2Fethnic%2Fpages%2FPage.cfm%3FLang%3DE%26Geo%3DPR%26Code%3D01%26Data%3DCount%26Table%3D2%26StartRec%3D1%26Sort%3D3%26Display%3DAll%26CSDFilter%3D5000&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABritish+people" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Bureau_of_Statistics" title="Australian Bureau of Statistics">Australian Bureau of Statistics</a> in 2011 showed that 7,238,500 Australians reported having English ancestry, and 1,792,600 reported having Scottish ancestry. The most commonly reported ancestry was English (36.1% of the population). Scottish ancestry was reported by 8.3% of the population. See: <br /> <cite id="CITEREFAustralian_Bureau_of_Statistics2012" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Australian_Bureau_of_Statistics" title="Australian Bureau of Statistics">Australian Bureau of Statistics</a> (21 June 2012), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.abs.gov.au/ausstats/abs@.nsf/Lookup/2071.0main+features902012-2013"><i>Reflecting a Nation: Stories from the 2011 Census, 2012\xe2\x80\x932013</i></a>, abs.gov.au<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 May</span> 2014</span></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Reflecting+a+Nation%3A+Stories+from+the+2011+Census%2C+2012%E2%80%932013&amp;rft.pub=abs.gov.au&amp;rft.date=2012-06-21&amp;rft.au=Australian+Bureau+of+Statistics&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.abs.gov.au%2Fausstats%2Fabs%40.nsf%2FLookup%2F2071.0main%2Bfeatures902012-2013&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABritish+people" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/> In 2006, 63% of the population had reported British ancestry. 2011 showed that 7,238,500 Australians reported having English ancestry, and 1,792,600 reported having Scottish ancestry. The most commonly reported ancestry was English (36.1% of the population). Scottish ancestry was reported by 8.3% of the population.
